</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ethanol fuel in Brazil</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg/220px-Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg/330px-Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg/440px-Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1476" data-file-height="1961" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Six_full_flex-fuel_Brazilian_automobiles_09_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Six typical <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel models</a> from several car makers, popularly called "flex" cars, that run on any blend of <a href="/wiki/Hydrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrous">hydrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_(fuel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethanol (fuel)">ethanol</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E20 to E25</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> is the world's second largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a>. Brazil and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> led the industrial production of ethanol fuel in 2014, together accounting for 83.4 percent of the world's production. In 2014 Brazil produced 23.4 billion liters (6.19 billion <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">U.S. liquid gallons</a>), representing 25.2 percent of the world's total ethanol used as fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2015_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFAProd2007_2014_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAProd2007_2014-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brazil is considered to have the world's first <a href="/wiki/Sustainable" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable">sustainable</a> <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> economy and the biofuel industry leader,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apollo_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Econon06_08_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econon06_08-6">[6]</a></sup> a policy model for other countries; and its sugarcane ethanol "the most successful <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuel</a> to date."<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-7">[7]</a></sup> However, some authors consider that the successful Brazilian ethanol model is sustainable only in Brazil due to its advanced <a href="/wiki/Agrobusiness" class="mw-redirect" title="Agrobusiness">agri-industrial</a> technology and its enormous amount of arable land available;<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-7">[7]</a></sup> while according to other authors it is a solution only for some countries in the tropical zone of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HotFlat_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HotFlat-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KSG2009_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSG2009-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brazil’s 40-year-old ethanol fuel program is based on the most efficient agricultural technology for sugarcane cultivation in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-IADB_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IADB-11">[11]</a></sup> uses modern equipment and cheap <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> as feedstock, the residual cane-waste (<a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>) is used to produce heat and power, which results in a very competitive price and also in a high <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">energy balance</a> (output energy/input energy), which varies from 8.3 for average conditions to 10.2 for best practice production.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MLA_2004_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_2004-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. EPA</a> designated Brazilian sugarcane ethanol as an <a href="/wiki/Indirect_land_use_change_impacts_of_biofuels#EPA_Renewable_Fuel_Standard" title="Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels">advanced biofuel</a> due to its 61% reduction of <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle assessment">total life cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a>, including <a href="/wiki/ILUC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">direct indirect land use change emissions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA2010_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2010-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GreenMom_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMom-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/220px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/330px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/440px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brazil has ethanol fuel available throughout the country. Shown here a typical <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">Petrobras</a> gas station at <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> with dual fuel service, marked A for <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">alcohol (ethanol)</a> and G for gasoline.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are no longer any light vehicles in Brazil running on pure gasoline. Since 1976 the government made it mandatory to blend <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> ethanol with gasoline, fluctuating between 10% to 22%.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-15">[15]</a></sup> and requiring just a minor adjustment on regular gasoline engines. In 1993 the mandatory blend was fixed by law at 22% anhydrous ethanol (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E22</a>) by volume in the entire country, but with leeway to the Executive to set different percentages of ethanol within pre-established boundaries. In 2003 these limits were set at a minimum of 20% and a maximum of 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-Lei8723_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lei8723-16">[16]</a></sup> Since July 1, 2007 the mandatory blend is 25% of anhydrous ethanol and 75% gasoline or <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E25" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E25 blend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2007_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2007-17">[17]</a></sup> The lower limit was reduced to 18% in April 2011 due to recurring ethanol supply shortages and high prices that take place between harvest seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-E18_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E18-18">[18]</a></sup> By mid March 2015 the government raised temporarily the ethanol blend in regular gasoline from 25% to 27%.<sup id="cite_ref-E27_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E27-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Brazilian car manufacturing industry developed <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a> that can run on any proportion of gasoline (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E20-E25 blend</a>) and hydrous ethanol (<a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-CEPAL_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CEPAL-20">[20]</a></sup> Introduced in the market in 2003, flex vehicles became a commercial success,<sup id="cite_ref-ICIS_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIS-21">[21]</a></sup> dominating the passenger vehicle market with a 94% <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> of all new cars and light vehicles sold in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-ICCT2015_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICCT2015-22">[22]</a></sup> By mid-2010 there were 70 flex models available in the market,<sup id="cite_ref-ModAvailable_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModAvailable-23">[23]</a></sup> and as of December 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, a total of 15 car manufacturers produce flex-fuel engines, dominating all light vehicle segments except sports cars, off-road vehicles and minivans.<sup id="cite_ref-ICCT2015_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICCT2015-22">[22]</a></sup> The cumulative production of flex-fuel cars and light commercial vehicles reached the milestone of 10 million vehicles in March 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Veja0310_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja0310-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCC0310_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0310-25">[25]</a></sup> and the 20&#160;million-unit milestone was reached in June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-BR20miFlex_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR20miFlex-26">[26]</a></sup> As of June 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, flex-fuel light-duty vehicle cumulative sales totaled 25.5&#160;million units,<sup id="cite_ref-BR25miFlex_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR25miFlex-27">[27]</a></sup> and production of flex motorcycles totaled 4&#160;million in March 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex4mi_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex4mi-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The success of "flex" vehicles, together with the mandatory E25 blend throughout the country, allowed ethanol fuel consumption in the country to achieve a 50% market share of the gasoline-powered fleet in February 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-ANP07_2008_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP07_2008-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50consumo_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50consumo-30">[30]</a></sup> In terms of <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">energy equivalent</a>, sugarcane ethanol represented 17.6% of the country's total energy consumption by the transport sector in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-BEN2009_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEN2009-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left; float:left; width:auto; background:none; margin-bottom:0.5em; padding:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0;" class="toclimit-3">

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="History of ethanol fuel in Brazil">History of ethanol fuel in Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicles_in_Brazil" title="Flexible-fuel vehicles in Brazil">Flexible-fuel vehicles in Brazil</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">Common ethanol fuel mixtures</a></div>

</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a> has been cultivated in Brazil since 1532 as sugar was one of the first <a href="/wiki/Commodities" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodities">commodities</a> exported to Europe by the Portuguese settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The first use of sugarcane ethanol as a fuel in Brazil dates back to the late twenties and early thirties of the twentieth century, with the introduction of the automobile in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-History_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-34">[34]</a></sup> Ethanol fuel production peaked during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and, as German submarine attacks threatened oil supplies, the mandatory blend became as high as 50% in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehistory_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehistory-35">[35]</a></sup> After the end of the war cheap oil caused gasoline to prevail, and ethanol blends were only used sporadically, mostly to take advantage of sugar surpluses,<sup id="cite_ref-Ehistory_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehistory-35">[35]</a></sup> until the seventies, when the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">first oil crisis</a> resulted in gasoline shortages and awareness of the dangers of oil dependence.<sup id="cite_ref-History_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ehistory_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehistory-35">[35]</a></sup> As a response to this crisis, the Brazilian government began promoting bioethanol as a fuel. The National Alcohol Program -<i>Pró-Álcool</i>- (<a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">'<b>Programa Nacional do Álcool'</b></span>), launched in 1975, was a nationwide program financed by the government to phase out automobile fuels derived from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>, such as gasoline, in favor of ethanol produced from <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bastos_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bastos-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veja1979_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja1979-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SeatleT_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeatleT-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fiat_147_br.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fiat_147_br.jpg/220px-Fiat_147_br.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fiat_147_br.jpg/330px-Fiat_147_br.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Fiat_147_br.jpg/440px-Fiat_147_br.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="371" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fiat_147_br.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1979 Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Fiat_147" title="Fiat 147">Fiat 147</a> was the first modern automobile launched to the market capable of running only on hydrous ethanol fuel (<a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first phase of the program concentrated on production of anhydrous ethanol for <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">blending with gasoline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-15">[15]</a></sup> The Brazilian government made mandatory the blending of ethanol fuel with gasoline, fluctuating from 1976 until 1992 between 10% to 22%.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-15">[15]</a></sup> Due to this mandatory minimum gasoline blend, pure gasoline (<a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E0</a>) is no longer sold in the country. A federal law was passed in October 1993 establishing a mandatory blend of 22% anhydrous ethanol (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E22</a>) in the entire country. This law also authorized the Executive to set different percentages of ethanol within pre-established boundaries; and since 2003 these limits were fixed at a maximum of 25% (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25</a>) and a minimum of 20% (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E20</a>) by volume.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lei8723_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lei8723-16">[16]</a></sup> Since then, the government has set the percentage of the ethanol blend according to the results of the <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> harvest and the levels of ethanol production from sugarcane, resulting in blend variations even within the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-USP_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USP-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png/300px-Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png" width="300" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png/450px-Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png/600px-Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png 2x" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="546" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_ethanol_and_flex_veh_in_Brazil_1979_2008.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Historical trend of Brazilian production of light vehicles by type of fuel, neat ethanol (alcohol), <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex fuel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> vehicles from 1979 to 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since July 2007 the mandatory blend is 25% of anhydrous ethanol and 75% gasoline or <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E25" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E25 blend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2007_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2007-17">[17]</a></sup> However, in 2010, and as a result of supply concerns and high ethanol fuel prices, the government mandated a temporary 90-day blend reduction from E25 to E20 beginning February 1, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2010_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2010-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> By mid March 2015 the government raised temporarily the ethanol blend in regular gasoline from 25% to 27%. The blend on premium gasoline was kept at 25% upon request by ANFAVEA, the Brazilian association of automakers, because of concerns about the effects on the higher blend on cars that were built for E25, as opposed to flex-fuel cars.<sup id="cite_ref-E27_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E27-19">[19]</a></sup> The government approved the higher blend as an economic incentive for ethanol producers, due to an existing overstock of over 1 billion liters (264 million US gallons) of ethanol. The implementation of E27 is expected to allow the consumption of the overstock before the end of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>After testing in government fleets with several prototypes developed by the local carmakers, and compelled by the <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">second oil crisis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_147" title="Fiat 147">Fiat 147</a>, the first modern commercial <a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol vehicle">neat ethanol car</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100 only</a>) was launched to the market in July 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-Veja1979_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja1979-37">[37]</a></sup> The Brazilian government provided three important initial drivers for the ethanol industry: guaranteed purchases by the state-owned oil company <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">Petrobras</a>, low-interest loans for agro-industrial ethanol firms, and fixed gasoline and ethanol prices where hydrous ethanol sold for 59% of the government-set gasoline price at the pump. Subsidising ethanol production in this manner and setting an artificially low price established ethanol as an alternative to gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-lovins_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lovins-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>After reaching more than 4 million cars and light trucks running on pure ethanol by the late 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2-43">[43]</a></sup> representing one third of the country's motor vehicle fleet,<sup id="cite_ref-Veja1989_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja1989-44">[44]</a></sup> ethanol production and sales of ethanol-only cars tumbled due to several factors. First, gasoline prices fell sharply as a result of the <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">1980s oil glut</a>, but mainly because of a shortage of ethanol fuel supply in the local market left thousands of vehicles in line at gas stations or out of fuel in their garages by mid-1989.<sup id="cite_ref-SeatleT_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeatleT-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veja1989_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja1989-44">[44]</a></sup> As supply could not keep pace with the increasing demand required by the now significant ethanol-only fleet, the Brazilian government began importing ethanol in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-IADB_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IADB-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CEPAL_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CEPAL-20">[20]</a></sup> Since 1979 until December 2010 <a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol vehicle">neat ethanol vehicles</a> totaled 5.7 million units.<sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA4_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA4-45">[45]</a></sup> The number of neat ethanol vehicles still in use was estimated between 2 and 3 million vehicles by 2003,<sup id="cite_ref-CEPAL_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CEPAL-20">[20]</a></sup> and estimated at 1.22 million as of December 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Frota092012_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frota092012-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg/220px-Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg/330px-Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg/440px-Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1883" data-file-height="1293" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brazilian_2003_VW_Gol_1.6_Total_Flex.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2003 <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Gol" title="Volkswagen Gol">VW Gol 1.6 Total Flex</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel car</a> capable of running on any blend of <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and ethanol.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Confidence on ethanol-powered vehicles was restored only with the introduction in the Brazilian market of <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a>. In March 2003 <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> launched in the Brazilian market the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Gol" title="Volkswagen Gol">Gol</a> 1.6 Total Flex, the first commercial <a href="/wiki/Flexible_fuel_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Flexible fuel vehicle">flexible fuel vehicle</a> capable of running on any blend of gasoline and ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> By 2010 manufacturers that build flexible fuel vehicles include <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiat" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiat">Fiat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peugeot" title="Peugeot">Peugeot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn" title="Citroën">Citroën</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kia_Motors" title="Kia Motors">Kia Motors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kia10_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kia10-52">[52]</a></sup> In 2013, Ford launched the first flex fuel car with direct injection: the Focus 2.0 Duratec Direct Flex.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Flexible fuel cars were 22% of the car sales in 2004, 73% in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune-54">[54]</a></sup> 87.6% in July 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-July2008_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-July2008-55">[55]</a></sup> and reached a record 94% in August 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-UnicaFlex09_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UnicaFlex09-56">[56]</a></sup> The cumulative production of flex-fuel cars and light commercial vehicles reached the milestone of 10 million vehicles in March 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Veja0310_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja0310-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCC0310_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0310-25">[25]</a></sup> and 15&#160;million in January 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Registrations of flex-fuel cars and light trucks represented 87.0% of all passenger and light duty vehicles sold in the country in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2013_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2013-58">[58]</a></sup> Production passed the 20&#160;million-unit mark in June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-BR20miFlex_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR20miFlex-26">[26]</a></sup> By the end of 2014, flex-fuel cars represented 54% of the Brazilian registered stock of light-duty vehicles, while gasoline only vehicles represented 34.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> As of June 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, flex-fuel light-duty vehicle cumulative sales totaled 25.5&#160;million units.<sup id="cite_ref-BR25miFlex_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR25miFlex-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rapid adoption and commercial success of "flex" vehicles, as they are popularly known, together with the mandatory blend of alcohol with gasoline as <a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25 fuel</a>, have increased ethanol consumption up to the point that by February 2008 a landmark in ethanol consumption was achieved when ethanol retail sales surpassed the 50% market share of the gasoline-powered fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-ANP07_2008_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP07_2008-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50consumo_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50consumo-30">[30]</a></sup> This level of ethanol fuel consumption had not been reached since the end of the 1980s, at the peak of the <i>Pró-Álcool</i> Program.<sup id="cite_ref-ANP07_2008_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP07_2008-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50consumo_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50consumo-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ANP02_2008_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP02_2008-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg/220px-3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg/330px-3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg/440px-3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2379" data-file-height="1159" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3_Views_Honda_Flex_Titan_CG_150_Mix_Fuel_Injection_06_2009_Itu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2009 <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> CG 150 Titan Mix was launched in the Brazilian market and became the first <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel</a> <a href="/wiki/Motorcycle" title="Motorcycle">motorcycle</a> sold in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/BioEthanol_for_Sustainable_Transport" title="BioEthanol for Sustainable Transport">BioEthanol for Sustainable Transport (BEST)</a> project, the first ethanol-powered (<a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#ED95" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">ED95</a>) bus began operations in São Paulo city in December 2007 as a one-year trial project.<sup id="cite_ref-SaoBEST_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SaoBEST-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> A second ED95 trial bus began operating in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> city in November 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Based on the satisfactory results obtained during the 3-year trial operation of the two buses, in November 2010 the municipal government of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> city signed an agreement with <a href="/wiki/UNICA,_Brazil" title="UNICA, Brazil">UNICA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosan" title="Cosan">Cosan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania</a> and <i>Viação Metropolitana</i>", the local bus operator, to introduced a fleet of 50 ethanol-powered ED95 buses by May 2011. The local government objective is for the city's entire bus fleet, which is made of 15,000 diesel-powered buses, to use only <a href="/wiki/Renewable_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable fuel">renewable fuels</a> by 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The first ethanol-powered buses were delivered in May 2011, and the 50 ethanol-powered ED95 buses are scheduled to begin regular service in São Paulo in June 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-EthanolBus1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EthanolBus1-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EthanolBus2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EthanolBus2-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another innovation of the Brazilian flexible-fuel technology was the development of flex-fuel <a href="/wiki/Motorcycles" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorcycles">motorcycles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Motoflex_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Motoflex-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aldo_Tizzani_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldo_Tizzani-70">[70]</a></sup> The first flex motorcycle was launched by <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> in March 2009. Produced by its Brazilian subsidiary Moto Honda da Amazônia, the CG 150 Titan Mix is sold for around US$2,700. In order to avoid cold start problems, the fuel tank must have at least 20% of gasoline at temperatures below 15&#160;°C (59&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In September 2009, Honda launched a second flexible-fuel motorcycle, the on-off road <a href="/w/index.php?title=Honda_NXR_150_Bros_Mix&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Honda NXR 150 Bros Mix (page does not exist)">NXR 150 Bros Mix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> By December 2010 both Honda flexible-fuel motorcycles had reached cumulative sales of 515,726 units, representing an 18.1% market share of the Brazilian new motorcycle sales in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex09_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex09-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex10_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex10-76">[76]</a></sup> Two other flex-fuel motorcycles manufactured by Honda were launched in October 2010 and January 2011, the GC 150 FAN and the Honda BIZ 125 Flex.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> During 2011 a total of 956,117 flex-fuel motorcycles were produced, raising its market share to 56.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex11_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex11-78">[78]</a></sup> Production reached the 2&#160;million mark in August 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex2mi_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex2mi-79">[79]</a></sup> Flexible-fuel motorcycle production passed the 3&#160;million-unit milestone in October 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex3mi_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex3mi-80">[80]</a></sup> and the 4&#160;million mark in March 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex4mi_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex4mi-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_and_production_indicators">Economic and production indicators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Economic and production indicators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; text-align:center; margin-left: 10px">
<tr>
<th colspan="11" style="text-align:center; background:#cfc;">Brazilian ethanol production<sup>(a)</sup><sup>(b)</sup> 2004–2011<sup id="cite_ref-RFAProd2007_2014_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAProd2007_2014-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFAstats2009_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAstats2009-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFA1E_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFA1E-83">[83]</a></sup><br />

<tr>
<td colspan="11"><small>Note: (a) 2004–06 for all grades. (b) 2007–10 ethanol fuel only.<sup id="cite_ref-RFA1E_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFA1E-83">[83]</a></sup></small></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Ethanol production in Brazil uses sugarcane as <a href="/wiki/Feedstock" class="mw-redirect" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a> and relies on first-generation technologies based on the use of the <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> content of sugarcane. Ethanol yield has grown 3.77% per year since 1975 and productivity gains have been based on improvements in the agricultural and industrial phases of the production process. Further improvements on best practices are expected to allow in the short to mid-term an average ethanol productivity of 9,000 <a href="/wiki/Liter" class="mw-redirect" title="Liter">liters</a> per <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were 378 ethanol plants operating in Brazil by July 2008, 126 dedicated to ethanol production and 252 producing both sugar and ethanol. There are 15 additional plants dedicated exclusively to sugar production.<sup id="cite_ref-EPE08_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPE08-85">[85]</a></sup> These plants have an installed capacity of crushing 538 million <a href="/wiki/Metric_ton" class="mw-redirect" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> of sugarcane per year, and there are 25 plants under construction expected to be on line by 2009 that will add an additional capacity of crushing 50 million tons of sugarcane per year.<sup id="cite_ref-EPE08_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPE08-85">[85]</a></sup> The typical plant costs approximately US$150 million and requires a nearby sugarcane plantation of 30,000 hectares.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png/250px-Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png" width="250" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png/375px-Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png/500px-Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png 2x" data-file-width="886" data-file-height="717" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_alcohol_production_by_ethanol_type_Brazil_1990_2008.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Production by harvest year 1990/91 to 2007/08.<sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats-86">[86]</a></sup> Green is <a href="/wiki/Hydrated" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrated">hydrated</a> ethanol (E100) and yellow is anhydrous ethanol use for <a href="/wiki/Gasohol" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasohol">gasohol</a> blending.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ethanol production is concentrated in the <a href="/wiki/Center-West_Region,_Brazil" class="mw-redirect" title="Center-West Region, Brazil">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Southeast Region, Brazil">Southeast</a> regions of the country, led by <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">São Paulo state</a>, with around 60% of the country's total ethanol production, followed by <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">Paraná</a> (8%), <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">Minas Gerais</a> (8%) and <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">Goiás</a> (5%).<sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats-86">[86]</a></sup> These two regions have been responsible for 90% of Brazil's ethanol production since 2005<sup id="cite_ref-MLA_2004_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_2004-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats-86">[86]</a></sup> and the harvest season goes from April to November. The <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">Northeast Region</a> is responsible for the remaining 10% of ethanol production, led by <a href="/wiki/Alagoas" title="Alagoas">Alagoas</a> with 2% of total production.<sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats-86">[86]</a></sup> The harvest season in the North-Northeast region goes from September to March, and the average productivity in this region is lower than the South-Central region.<sup id="cite_ref-CONAB_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CONAB-87">[87]</a></sup> Due to the difference in the two main harvest seasons, Brazilian statistics for sugar and ethanol production are commonly reported on a harvest two-year basis rather than on a calendar year.</p>
<p>For the 2008/09 harvest it is expected that about 44% of the sugarcane will be used for sugar, 1% for <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a>, and 55% for ethanol production.<sup id="cite_ref-Folha_04_08_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folha_04_08-88">[88]</a></sup> An estimate of between 24.9 billion <a href="/wiki/Litres" class="mw-redirect" title="Litres">litres</a> (6.58 billion <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">U.S. liquid gallons</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-EPE08_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPE08-85">[85]</a></sup> to 27.1 billion litres (7.16 billion gallons)<sup id="cite_ref-CONAB_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CONAB-87">[87]</a></sup> of ethanol are expected to be produced in 2008/09 harvest year, with most of the production being destined for the internal market, and only 4.2 billion liters (1.1 billion gallons) for exports, with an estimated 2.5 billion liters (660 million gallons) destined for the US market.<sup id="cite_ref-Folha_04_08_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folha_04_08-88">[88]</a></sup> Sugarcane cultivated area grew from 7 million to 7.8 million hectares of land from 2007 to 2008, mainly using abandoned pasture lands.<sup id="cite_ref-Folha_04_08_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folha_04_08-88">[88]</a></sup> In 2008 Brazil has 276 million hectares of arable land, 72% use for pasture, 16.9% for grain crops, and 2.8% for sugarcane, meaning that ethanol is just requiring approximately 1.5% of all arable land available in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Folha_04_08_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folha_04_08-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>As sugar and ethanol share the same feedstock and their industrial processing is fully integrated, formal employment statistics are usually presented together. In 2000 there were 642,848 workers employed by these industries, and as ethanol production expanded, by 2005 there were 982,604 workers employed in the sugarcane cultivation and industrialization, including 414,668 workers in the sugarcane fields, 439,573 workers in the sugar mills, and 128,363 workers in the ethanol distilleries.<sup id="cite_ref-Labor_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labor-89">[89]</a></sup> While employment in the ethanol distilleries grew 88.4% from 2000 to 2005, employment in the sugar fields just grew 16.2% as a direct result of expansion of mechanical harvest instead manual harvesting, which avoids burning the sugarcane fields before manual cutting and also increases productivity. The states with the most employment in 2005 were São Paulo (39.2%), <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">Pernambuco</a> (15%), Alagoas (14.1%), Paraná (7%), and Minas Gerais (5.6%).<sup id="cite_ref-Labor_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labor-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2009.E2.80.932014_crisis">2009–2014 crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2009–2014 crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since 2009 the Brazilian ethanol industry has experienced a crisis due to multiple causes. They include the <a href="/wiki/Economic_crisis_of_2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic crisis of 2008">economic crisis of 2008</a>; poor sugarcane harvests due to unfavorable weather; high sugar prices in the world market that made more attractive to produce sugar rather than ethanol;<sup id="cite_ref-BW0012012_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW0012012-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Econom012012_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econom012012-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EP112011_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EP112011-92">[92]</a></sup> a freeze imposed by the Brazilian government on the petrol and diesel prices.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature2012_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2012-93">[93]</a></sup> Brazilian ethanol fuel production in 2011 was 21.1 billion liters (5.6 billion U.S. liquid gallons), down from 26.2 billion liters (6.9 billion gallons) in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2012_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2012-81">[81]</a></sup> while in 2012 the production of ethanol was 26% lower than in 2008. By 2012 a total of 41 ethanol plants out of about 400 have closed and the sugar-cane crop yields dropped from 115 tonnes per hectare in 2008 to 69 tonnes per hectare in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature2012_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2012-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>A supply shortage took place for several months during 2010 and 2011, and prices climbed to the point that ethanol fuel was no longer attractive for owners of flex-fuel vehicles; the government reduced the minimum ethanol blend in gasoline to reduce demand and keep ethanol fuel prices from rising further; and for the first time since the 1990s, (corn) ethanol fuel was imported from the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-BW0012012_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW0012012-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Econom012012_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econom012012-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EP112011_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EP112011-92">[92]</a></sup> The imports totaled around 1.5 billion litres in 2011–2012. The ethanol share in the transport fuel market decreased from 55% in 2008 to 35% in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature2012_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2012-93">[93]</a></sup> As a result of higher ethanol prices combined with government subsidies to keep gasoline price lower than the international market value, by November 2013 only 23% flex-fuel car owners were using ethanol regularly, down from 66% in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2014 Brazil produced 23.4 billion liters (6.19 billion U.S. liquid gallons) of ethanol fuel, however, during that year Brazil imported ethanol from the United States, ranking as the second largest U.S. export market in 2014 after Canada, and representing about 13% of total American exports.<sup id="cite_ref-RFAOutlook2015_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAOutlook2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RFAProd2007_2014_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFAProd2007_2014-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_technology">Agricultural technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Agricultural technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/220px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/330px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/440px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1880" data-file-height="1253" /></a>

Ethanol fuel ready for distribution, <a href="/wiki/Piracicaba" title="Piracicaba">Piracicaba</a>, São Paulo State.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A key aspect for the development of the ethanol industry in Brazil was the investment in agricultural <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> by both the public and private sector.<sup id="cite_ref-IADB_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IADB-11">[11]</a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/EMBRAPA" class="mw-redirect" title="EMBRAPA">EMBRAPA</a>, the state-owned company in charge for applied research on agriculture, together with research developed by state institutes and universities, especially in the State of São Paulo, have allowed Brazil to become a major innovator in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and agronomic practices,<sup id="cite_ref-EMBRAPA_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMBRAPA-96">[96]</a></sup> resulting in the most efficient agricultural technology for sugarcane cultivation in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-IADB_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IADB-11">[11]</a></sup> Efforts have been concentrated in increasing the efficiency of inputs and processes to optimize output per hectare of feedstock, and the result has been a threefold increase of sugarcane yields in 29 years, as Brazilian average ethanol yields went from 2,024 liters per ha in 1975 to 5,917 liters per ha in 2004; allowing the efficiency of ethanol production to grow at a rate of 3.77% per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> Brazilian biotechnologies include the development of sugarcane varieties that have a larger sugar or energy content, one of the main drivers for high yields of ethanol per unit of planted area. The increase of the index total recoverable sugar (TRS) from sugarcane has been very significant, 1.5% per year in the period 1977 to 2004, resulting in an increase from 95 to 140&#160;kg/ha.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> Innovations in the industrial process have allowed an increase in sugar extraction in the period 1977 to 2003. The average annual improvement was 0.3%; some mills have already reached extraction efficiencies of 98%.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biotechnology research and genetic improvement have led to the development of strains that are more resistant to disease, bacteria, and pests, and also have the capacity to respond to different environments, thus allowing the expansion of sugarcane cultivation to areas previously considered inadequate for such cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-EMBRAPA_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMBRAPA-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup> By 2008 more than 500 sugarcane varieties are cultivated in Brazil, and 51 of them were released just during the last ten years. Four research programs, two private and two public, are devoted to further genetic improvement.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup> Since the mid nineties, Brazilian biotechnology laboratories have developed <a href="/wiki/Transgenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic">transgenic</a> varieties, still non commerciallized. Identification of 40,000 cane genes was completed in 2003 and there are a couple dozen research groups working on the functional <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>, still on the experimental phase, but commercial results are expected within five years.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also, there is ongoing research regarding sugarcane biological <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> fixation, with the most promising plant varieties showing yields three times the national average in soils of very low fertility, thus avoiding nitrogenous fertilization.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> There is also research for the development of second-generation or <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IADB_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IADB-11">[11]</a></sup> In São Paulo state an increase of 12% in sugar cane yield and 6.4% in sugar content is expected over the next decade. This advance combined with an expected 6.2% improvement in fermentation efficiency and 2% in sugar extraction, may increase ethanol yields by 29%, raising average ethanol productivity to 9,000 liters/ha.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> Approximately US$50 million has recently been allocated for research and projects focused on advancing the obtention of ethanol from sugarcane in São Paulo state.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_process">Production process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">Sucrose</a> extracted from sugarcane accounts for little more than 30% of the chemical energy stored in the harvested parts of the mature plant; 35% is in the leaves and stem tips, which are left in the fields during harvest, and 35% are in the fibrous material (<a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>) left over from pressing. Most of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial process">industrial processing</a> of sugarcane in Brazil is done through a very integrated production chain, allowing sugar production, industrial ethanol processing, and electricity generation from byproducts.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> The typical steps for large-scale production of sugar and ethanol include <a href="/wiki/Mill_(grinding)" title="Mill (grinding)">milling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> of ethanol, and <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Milling_and_refining">Milling and refining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Milling and refining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a></div>
<p>Once harvested, sugarcane is usually transported to the plant by <a href="/wiki/Semi-trailer_truck" title="Semi-trailer truck">semi-trailer trucks</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Quality_control" title="Quality control">quality control</a>, sugarcane is washed, chopped, and shredded by revolving knives; the feedstock is fed to and extracted by a set of mill combinations to collect a juice, called <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane_juice" title="Sugarcane juice">garapa</a> in Brazil, that contain 10–15% sucrose, and <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>, the fiber residue. The main objective of the milling process is to extract the largest possible amount of sucrose from the cane, and a secondary but important objective is the production of bagasse with a low moisture content as boiler fuel, as bagasse is burned for electricity generation (see <a href="#Electricity_generation_from_bagasse">below</a>), allowing the plant to be self-sufficient in energy and to generate electricity for the local power grid.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> The cane juice or garapa is then filtered and treated by chemicals and <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurized</a>. Before <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, the juice is filtered once again, producing <a href="/wiki/Vinasse" title="Vinasse">vinasse</a>, a fluid rich in organic compounds. The syrup resulting from evaporation is then <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a> producing a mixture of clear crystals surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge" title="Centrifuge">centrifuge</a> is used to separate the sugar from molasses, and the crystals are washed by addition of steam, after which the crystals are dried by an airflow. Upon cooling, sugar crystallizes out of the syrup.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> From this point, the sugar <a href="/wiki/Refining" title="Refining">refining</a> process continues to produce different grades of sugar, and the molasses continue a separate process to produce ethanol.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation.2C_distillation_and_dehydration">Fermentation, distillation and dehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fermentation, distillation and dehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">Ethanol fermentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azeotropic_distillation" title="Azeotropic distillation">Azeotropic distillation</a></div>
<p>The resulting molasses are treated to become a sterilized molasse free of impurities, ready to be fermented. In the <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a> process sugars are transformed into ethanol by addition of <a href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeast (wine)">yeast</a>. Fermentation time varies from four to twelve hours resulting in an alcohol content of 7-10% by total volume (<a href="/wiki/Degrees_Gay-Lussac" class="mw-redirect" title="Degrees Gay-Lussac">°GL</a>), called fermented wine. The yeast is recovered from this wine through a centrifuge. Making use of the different boiling points the alcohol in the fermented wine is separated from the main resting solid components. The remaining product is hydrated ethanol with a concentration of 96°GL,<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> the highest concentration of ethanol that can be achieved via <a href="/wiki/Azeotropic" class="mw-redirect" title="Azeotropic">azeotropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, and by national specification can contain up to 4.9% of water by volume.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> This hydrous ethanol is the fuel used by ethanol-only and <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex</a> vehicles in the country. Further dehydration is normally done by addition of chemicals, up to the specified 99.7°GL in order to produce anhydrous ethanol,<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> which is used for blending with pure gasoline to obtain the country's <a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25</a> mandatory blend.<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2007_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2007-17">[17]</a></sup> The additional processing required to convert hydrated into anhydrous ethanol increases the cost of the fuel, as in 2007 the average producer price difference between the two was around 14% for São Paulo State.<sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats2_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats2-95">[95]</a></sup> This production price difference, though small, contributes to the competitiveness of the hydrated ethanol (<a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100</a>) used in Brazil, not only with regard to local gasoline prices but also as compared to other countries such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, that only use anhydrous ethanol for their flex fuel fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethanol_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethanol-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity_generation_from_bagasse">Electricity generation from bagasse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Electricity generation from bagasse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bioenergy" title="Bioenergy">Bioenergy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG/220px-Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG/330px-Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG/440px-Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="909" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neat_ethanol_car_Piracicaba_05_2009_5664.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Neat ethanol car fueling <a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100</a> at a <a href="/wiki/Piracicaba" title="Piracicaba">Piracicaba</a> gas station, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">São Paulo</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the early days, <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a> was burnt in the plant to provide the energy required for the industrial part of the process. Today, the Brazilian best practice uses high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boilers</a> that increases energy recovery, allowing most sugar-ethanol plants to be energetically self-sufficient and even sell surplus electricity to utilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> By 2000, the total amount of sugarcane bagasse produced per year was 50 million tons/<a href="/wiki/Dry_basis" title="Dry basis">dry basis</a> out of more than 300 million tons of harvested sugarcane. Several authors estimated a potential power generation from the use of sugarcane bagasse ranging from 1,000 to 9,000 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a>, depending on the technology used and the use of harvest trash. One utility in São Paulo is buying more than 1% of its electricity from sugar mills, with a production capacity of 600 MW for self-use and 100 MW for sale.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> According to analysis from Frost &amp; Sullivan, Brazil's sugarcane bagasse used for power generation has reached 3.0 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> in 2007, and it is expected to reach 12.2 GW in 2014. The analysis also found that sugarcane bagasse cogeneration accounts for 3% of the total Brazilian energy matrix.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The energy is especially valuable to utilities because it is produced mainly in the dry season when hydroelectric dams are running low.</p>
<p>According to a study commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> government in 2006 to evaluate the sustainability of Brazilian bioethanol "<i>...there are also substantial gains possible in the efficiency of electricity use and generation: The electricity used for distillery operations has been estimated at 12.9 kWh/tonne cane, with a best available technology rate of 9.6 kWh/tonne cane . For electricity generation the efficiency could be increased from 18 kWh/tonne cane presently, to 29.1 kWh/tonne cane maximum. The production of surplus electricity could in theory be increased from 5.3 kWh/tonne cane to 19 kWh/tonne cane.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_generation_from_ethanol">Electric generation from ethanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Electric generation from ethanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Brazil has several experimental programs for the production of electricity using sugar cane ethanol as fuel. A joint venture of <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">Petrobras</a> is operating one commercial pilot plant in <a href="/wiki/Juiz_de_Fora" title="Juiz de Fora">Juiz de Fora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">Minas Gerais</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overall_energy_use">Overall energy use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Overall energy use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg/220px-Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg/330px-Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg/440px-Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zona_da_Mata_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sugarcane plantation in the State of <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">Pernambuco</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Energy-use associated with the production of sugarcane ethanol derives from three primary sources: the agricultural sector, the industrial sector, and the distribution sector. In the agricultural sector, 35.98 GJ of energy are used to plant, maintain, and harvest one hectare (10,000 m2) of sugarcane for usable biofuel. This includes energy from numerous inputs, including nitrogen, phosphate, potassium oxide, lime, seed, herbicides, insecticides, labor and diesel fuel. The industrial sector, which includes the milling and refining sugarcane and the production of ethanol fuel, uses 3.63 GJ of energy and generates 155.57 GJ of energy per hectare of sugarcane plantation. Scientists estimate that the potential power generated from the cogeneration of bagasse could range from 1,000 to 9,000 MW, depending on harvest and technology factors. In Brazil, this is about 3% of the total energy needed. The burning of bagasse can generate 18 kilowatt-hours, or 64.7 MJ per Mg of sugarcane. Distillery facilities require about 45 MJ to operate, leaving a surplus energy supply of 19.3 MJ, or 5.4 kWh. In terms of distribution, researchers calculates sugarcane ethanol’s transport energy requirement to be .44 GJ per cubic-meter, thus one hectare of land would require 2.82 GJ of energy for successful transport and distribution. After taking all three sectors into account, the EROEI (Energy Return over Energy Invested) for sugarcane ethanol is about 8.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are several improvements to the industrial processes, such as adopting a <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> process to produce ethanol instead of surplus electricity, or the use of advanced boiler and turbine technology to increase the electricity yield, or a higher use of excess bagasse and harvest trash currently left behind in the fields, that together with various other efficiency improvements in sugarcane farming and the distribution chain have the potential to allow further efficiency increases, translating into higher yields, lower production costs, and also further improvements in the <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">energy balance</a> and the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
