<p>Brazil is the world's largest exporter of ethanol. In 2007 it exported 933.4 million gallons (3,532.7 million liters),<sup id="cite_ref-MAPA07_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAPA07-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EUx_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUx-110">[110]</a></sup> representing almost 20% of its production, and accounting for almost 50% of the global exports.<sup id="cite_ref-SWE_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWE-113">[113]</a></sup> Since 2004 Brazilian exporters have as their main customers the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_%26_Tobago" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinidad &amp; Tobago">Trinidad &amp; Tobago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SWE_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWE-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>The countries in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Caribbean Basin">Caribbean Basin</a> import relative high quantities of Brazilian ethanol, but not much is destined for domestic consumption. These countries reprocess the product, usually converting Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Hydrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrous">hydrated</a> ethanol into <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> ethanol, and then re-export it to the United States, gaining value-added and avoiding the 2.5% duty and the <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.54</span> per gallon <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariff</a>, thanks to the trade agreements and benefits granted by <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin_Initiative" title="Caribbean Basin Initiative">Caribbean Basin Initiative (CBI)</a>. This process is limited by a quota, set at 7% of U.S. ethanol consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-CBI_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBI-114">[114]</a></sup> Although direct U.S. exports fell in 2007, imports from four CBI countries almost doubled, increasing from 15.5% in 2006 to 25.8% in 2007, reflecting increasing re-exports to the U.S., thus partially compensating the loss of Brazilian direct exports to the U.S. This situation has caused some concerns in the United States, as it and Brazil are trying to build a partnership to increase ethanol production in Latin American and the Caribbean. As the U.S. is encouraging "new ethanol production in other countries, production that could directly compete with U.S.-produced ethanol".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S., potentially the largest market for Brazilian ethanol imports, currently imposes a tariff on Brazilian ethanol of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.54</span> per gallon in order to encourage domestic ethanol production and protect the budding ethanol industry in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Historically, this tariff was intended to offset the 45 cent per gallon blender's federal <a href="/wiki/Tax_credit" title="Tax credit">tax credit</a> that is applied to ethanol no matter its country of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tariff54c_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tariff54c-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaxCredit1_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaxCredit1-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaxCredit2_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaxCredit2-119">[119]</a></sup> Exports of Brazilian ethanol to the U.S. reached a total of US$1 billion in 2006, an increase of 1,020% over 2005 (US$98 millions),<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> but fell significantly in 2007 due to sharp increases in American ethanol production from <a href="/wiki/Corn" class="mw-redirect" title="Corn">corn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> As shown in the table, the United States remains the largest single importer of Brazilian ethanol exports, though collectively the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin_Initiative" title="Caribbean Basin Initiative">CBI countries</a> now import a similar amount.<sup id="cite_ref-MAPA07_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAPA07-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MAPA06_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAPA06-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 study by <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University's</a> Center for Agricultural and Rural Development found that removing the U.S. import tariff would result in less than 5% of the United States’ ethanol being imported from Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-Costs_and_Benefits_to_Taxpayers.2C_Consumers.2C_and_Producers_from_U.S._Ethanol_Policies_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costs_and_Benefits_to_Taxpayers.2C_Consumers.2C_and_Producers_from_U.S._Ethanol_Policies-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Little_impact_if_U.S._ethanol_tax_breaks_end:_Study_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_impact_if_U.S._ethanol_tax_breaks_end:_Study-124">[124]</a></sup> Also a 2010 study by the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">Congressional Budget Office</a> (CBO) found that the costs to American taxpayers of using a biofuel to reduce gasoline consumption by one gallon are $1.78 for corn ethanol and $3.00 for cellulosic ethanol. In a similar way, and without considering potential <a href="/wiki/ILUC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">indirect land use effects</a>, the costs to taxpayers of reducing <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> through tax credits are about $750 per metric ton of <a href="/wiki/CO2-equivalent" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2-equivalent">CO2-equivalent</a> for ethanol and around $275 per metric ton for cellulosic ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-CBO2010_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBO2010-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>After being renewed several times, the tax credit is set to expire on December 31, 2011, and both the <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.54</span> per gallon tariff and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.45</span> per gallon blender’s credit have been the subject of contentious debate in Washington, D.C. with ethanol interest groups and politicians staking positions on both sides of the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Growth_Energy_proposes_shift_in_fuel_policy_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Growth_Energy_proposes_shift_in_fuel_policy-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-After_30_years_of_federal_subsidies.2C_ethanol_can_go_it_alone_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-After_30_years_of_federal_subsidies.2C_ethanol_can_go_it_alone-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ag_leaders.2C_RFA_push_for_VEETC_extension_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ag_leaders.2C_RFA_push_for_VEETC_extension-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Path_Forward_for_Biofuels_Incentives_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Path_Forward_for_Biofuels_Incentives-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grassley.2C_Conrad_work_to_extend_ethanol_tax_incentives_to_build_energy_security_and_create_jobs_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grassley.2C_Conrad_work_to_extend_ethanol_tax_incentives_to_build_energy_security_and_create_jobs-130">[130]</a></sup> On June 16, 2011, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a> approved an amendment to the economic development bill to repeal both the tax credit and the tariff on ethanol, and though this bill has an uncertain future, it is considered a signal that the tax credits will not be renew when they expire at the end of 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist062311_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist062311-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT061711_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT061711-132">[132]</a></sup> The eventual elimination of the import tariff is not expect to have significant effects in the short term. That is because the Brazilian ethanol industry has been having trouble meeting its own domestic demand for ethanol during 2010 and 2011, and actually Brazil imported some corn ethanol from the U.S. The shortage in supply is due in part to high sugar prices, which make it more profitable for Brazilian producers to sell it as sugar than convert it to ethanol fuel. Also, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Credit_crunch" title="Credit crunch">credit crunch</a> caused by the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">financial crisis of 2007–2010</a>, the expansion of the Brazilian ethanol industry has not being able keep up pace with the accelerated growth of the <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex fuel fleet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT061711_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT061711-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crisis01_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crisis01-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crisis02_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crisis02-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. EPA</a>'s 2010 final ruling for the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Fuel_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable Fuel Standard">Renewable Fuel Standard</a> designated Brazilian sugarcane ethanol as an <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_fuel_standard#EPA_Renewable_Fuel_Standard" title="Low-carbon fuel standard">advanced biofuel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-EPA2010_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2010-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPArule2010_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPArule2010-135">[135]</a></sup> Brazilian ethanol producers hope this classification will contribute to lift import tariffs both in the U.S. and the rest of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-UNICA022010_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICA022010-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Also they expect to increase exports to the U.S., as the blending mandate requires an increasing quota of advanced biofuels, which is not likely to be fulfilled with cellulosic ethanol, and then it would force blenders to import more Brazilian sugarcane-based ethanol, despite the existing 54¢ per gallon tariff on ethanol imported directly from Brazil, or duty-free from the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin_Initiative" title="Caribbean Basin Initiative">CBI</a> countries that convert Brazilian hydrated ethanol into anhydrous ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prices_and_effect_on_oil_consumption">Prices and effect on oil consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Prices and effect on oil consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg/220px-Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg/330px-Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg/440px-Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="752" data-file-height="409" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fuel_prices_at_Rio_%26_Sao_Paulo_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alcohol and <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_prices" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasoline prices">gasoline prices</a> per liter at <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> (right), corresponding to a price ratio of <a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol fuel">E100 ethanol</a> to <a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25 gasoline</a> of 0.64 and 0.56.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png/220px-Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png/330px-Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png/440px-Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="821" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Historical_trend_ethanol_production_by_region_Brazil_1990_2008.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Historical variation of ethanol production by region from 1990/91 to 2006/07 (harvest year).<sup id="cite_ref-UNICAstats_86-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICAstats-86">[86]</a></sup> Light green is the production for the State of São Paulo.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most automobiles in Brazil run either on hydrous alcohol (<a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">E100</a>) or on gasohol (<a href="/wiki/E20_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="E20 fuel">E25 blend</a>), as the mixture of 25% anhydrous ethanol with gasoline is mandatory in the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-Portaria2007_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portaria2007-17">[17]</a></sup> Since 2003, dual-fuel ethanol <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex vehicles</a> that run on any proportion of hydrous ethanol and gasoline have been gaining popularity. These have electronic sensors that detect the type of fuel and adjust the engine combustion to match, so users can choose the cheapest available fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-3">[3]</a></sup> Sales of flex fuel vehicles reached 9.3 million by December 2009, representing 39% of the passenger vehicle fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-UnicaFlex10_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UnicaFlex10-140">[140]</a></sup> By mid-2010 there were 70 flex models available in the market<sup id="cite_ref-ModAvailable_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModAvailable-23">[23]</a></sup> and production by December 2010 reached more than 12.5 million flex vehicles including more than 500 thousand flex fuel motorcycles.<sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA2_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA2-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ANFAVEA4_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANFAVEA4-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex09_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex09-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MotoFlex10_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MotoFlex10-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the lower energy content of ethanol fuel, full flex-fuel vehicles get fewer miles per gallon. Ethanol price has to be between 25-30% cheaper per gallon to reach the break even point.<sup id="cite_ref-Apollo_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apollo-4">[4]</a></sup> As a <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_thumb" title="Rule of thumb">rule of thumb</a>, Brazilian consumers are frequently advised by the media to use more alcohol than gasoline in their mix only when ethanol prices are 30% lower or more than gasoline, as ethanol price fluctuates heavily depending on the harvest yields and seasonal fluctuation of sugarcane harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2005, ethanol prices have been very competitive without subsidies,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-3">[3]</a></sup> even with gasoline prices kept constant in local currency since mid-2005,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> at a time when oil was just approaching <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$60</span> a barrel. However, Brazilian gasoline taxes are high, around 54 percent,<sup id="cite_ref-FolhaGasoline_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FolhaGasoline-144">[144]</a></sup> while ethanol fuel taxes are lower and vary between 12% to 30%, depending of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> As of October 2008 the average price of E25 gasoline was $4.39 per gallon<sup id="cite_ref-ANP1_10_08_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP1_10_08-146">[146]</a></sup> while the average price for ethanol was US$2.69 per gallon.<sup id="cite_ref-ANP2_10_08_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP2_10_08-147">[147]</a></sup> This differential in taxation favors ethanol fuel consumption, and by the end of July 2008, when <a href="/wiki/2003_to_2008_world_oil_market_chronology" class="mw-redirect" title="2003 to 2008 world oil market chronology">oil prices</a> were close to its latest peak and the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_real" title="Brazilian real">Brazilian real</a> exchange rate to the US dollar was close to its most recent minimum, the average gasoline retail price at the pump in Brazil reached US$6.00 per gallon.<sup id="cite_ref-FolhaGasoline_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FolhaGasoline-144">[144]</a></sup> The price ratio between gasoline and ethanol fuel has been well above 30 percent during this period for most states, except during low sugar cane supply between harvests and for states located far away from the ethanol production centers. According to Brazilian producers, ethanol can remain competitive if the price of oil does not fall below US$30 a barrel.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2008 consumption of ethanol fuel by the Brazilian fleet of light vehicles, as pure ethanol and in gasohol, is replacing gasoline at the rate of about 27,000 cubic meters per day, and by February 2008 the combined consumption of anhydrous and hydrated ethanol fuel surpassed 50 percent of the fuel that would be needed to run the light vehicle fleet on pure gasoline alone. Monthly consumption of anhydrous ethanol for the mandatory E25 blend, together with hydrous ethanol used by flex vehicles, reached 1.432 billion liters, while pure gasoline consumption was 1.411 billion liters.<sup id="cite_ref-ANP07_2008_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANP07_2008-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50consumo_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50consumo-30">[30]</a></sup> Despite this volumetric parity, when expressed in terms of energy equivalent (<a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">toe</a>), sugarcane ethanol represented 17.6 percent of the country's total energy consumption by the transport sector in 2008, while gasoline represented 23.3 percent and diesel 49.2 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-BEN2009_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BEN2009-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the first time since 2003 sales of hydrous ethanol fell in 2010, with a decrease of 8.5 percent as compared to 2009. Total consumption of both hydrous and anhydrous ethanol fell by 2.9 percent while gasoline consumption increased by 17.5 percent. Despite the reduction in ethanol consumption, total ethanol sales reached 22.2 billion liters while pure gasoline consumption was 22.7 billion liters, keeping the market share for each fuel close to 50 percent. The decrease in hydrous ethanol consumption was due mainly to high sugar prices in the international markets, which reached a 30-year high in 2010. This peak in sugar prices caused sugarcane processing plants to produce more sugar than ethanol, and as supply contracted, E100 prices increased to the point that several times during 2010 the price of hydrous ethanol was less than 30 percent cheaper than gasoline. Another factor that contributed to this shift was the increase sales of imported gasoline only vehicles that took place during 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT061711_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT061711-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crisis01_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crisis01-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Crisis02_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crisis02-134">[134]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_the_United_States">Comparison with the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Comparison with the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brazil's sugar cane-based industry is more efficient than the U.S. corn-based industry. Sugar cane ethanol has an <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">energy balance</a> seven times greater than ethanol produced from corn.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-3">[3]</a></sup> Brazilian distillers are able to produce ethanol for 22 cents per liter, compared with the 30 cents per liter for corn-based ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> U.S. corn-derived ethanol costs 30% more because the corn starch must first be converted to sugar before being distilled into alcohol.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethanol_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethanol-104">[104]</a></sup> Despite this cost differential in production, the U.S. did not import more Brazilian ethanol because of U.S. trade barriers corresponding to a <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariff</a> of 54-cent per gallon, first imposed in 1980, but kept to offset the 45-cent per gallon blender's federal <a href="/wiki/Tax_credit" title="Tax credit">tax credit</a> that is applied to ethanol no matter its country of origin.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tariff54c_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tariff54c-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaxCredit1_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaxCredit1-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaxCredit2_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaxCredit2-119">[119]</a></sup> In 2011 the U.S. Congress decided not to extend the tariff and the tax credit, and as a result both ended on December 31, 2011. During these three decades the ethanol industry was awarded an estimated <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$45</span> billion in subsidies and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6</span> billion just in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-USTariff_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USTariff-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sugarcane cultivation requires a tropical or subtropical climate, with a minimum of 600&#160;mm (24&#160;in) of annual rainfall. Sugarcane is one of the most <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_efficiency" title="Photosynthetic efficiency">efficient photosynthesizers</a> in the plant kingdom, able to convert up to 2% of incident solar energy into biomass. Sugarcane production in the United States occurs in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>. The first three plants to produce sugarcane-based ethanol are expected to go online in Louisiana by mid-2009. Sugar mill plants in Lacassine, <a href="/wiki/St._James_Parish,_Louisiana" title="St. James Parish, Louisiana">St. James</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bunkie,_Louisiana" title="Bunkie, Louisiana">Bunkie</a> were converted to sugar cane-based ethanol production using <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombian</a> technology in order to make possible a profitable ethanol production. These three plants will produce 100 million gallons (378.5 million liters) of ethanol within five years.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> By 2009 two other sugarcane ethanol production projects are being developed in <a href="/wiki/Kauai" title="Kauai">Kauai</a>, Hawaii and <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Valley" title="Imperial Valley">Imperial Valley</a>, California.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol_diplomacy">Ethanol diplomacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ethanol diplomacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg/220px-Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg/330px-Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg/440px-Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3506" data-file-height="2337" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bushlulapetrobras09032007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Presidents <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> during Bush's visit to Brazil, March 2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Etanolbuss,_kungen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg/220px-Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg/330px-Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg/440px-Etanolbuss%2C_kungen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="2956" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Etanolbuss,_kungen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a> and King <a href="/wiki/Carl_XVI_Gustaf_of_Sweden" title="Carl XVI Gustaf of Sweden">Carl XVI Gustaf of Sweden</a> inspecting one of the 400 buses running on <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#ED95" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">ED95</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 2007, "ethanol diplomacy" was the focus of President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>'s Latin American tour, in which he and Brazil's president, <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a>, were seeking to promote the production and use of sugar cane–based ethanol throughout <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>. The two countries also agreed to share technology and set international standards for biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-WasPos1_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WasPos1-169">[169]</a></sup> The Brazilian sugar cane technology transfer will permit various <a href="/wiki/Central_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American">Central American</a> countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> countries, and various <a href="/wiki/Andean_Community_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean Community of Nations">Andean Countries</a> tariff-free trade with the U.S. thanks to existing concessionary trade agreements.</p>
<p>Even though the U.S. has imposed a US$0.54 tariff on every gallon of imported ethanol since 1980,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tariff54c_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tariff54c-117">[117]</a></sup> the Caribbean nations and Central American countries are exempt from such duties based on the benefits granted by the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin_Initiative" title="Caribbean Basin Initiative">Caribbean Basin Initiative (CBI)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CBI_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBI-114">[114]</a></sup> CBI provisions allow tariff-free access to the US market from ethanol produced from foreign feedstock (outside CBI countries) up to 7% of the previous year US consumption. Also additional quotas are allowed if the beneficiary countries produce at least 30% of the ethanol from local feedstocks up to an additional 35 million gallons (132.5 million liters).<sup id="cite_ref-CAFTA_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAFTA-170">[170]</a></sup> Thus, several countries have been importing hydrated ethanol from Brazil, processing it at local distilleries to <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydrate</a> it, and then re-exporting it as <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous_ethanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Anhydrous ethanol">anhydrous ethanol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> American farmers have complained about this loophole to legally bypass the tariff.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The 2005 <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American Free Trade Agreement">Dominican Republic – Central America Free Trade Agreement (CAFTA)</a> maintained the benefits granted by the CBI, and CAFTA provisions established country-specific shares for Costa Rica and El Salvador within the overall quota. An initial annual allowance was established for each country, with gradually increasing annual levels of access to the US market.<sup id="cite_ref-CAFTA_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAFTA-170">[170]</a></sup> The expectation is that using Brazilian technology for refining sugar cane–based ethanol, such countries could become net exporters to the United States in the short-term.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> In August 2007, Brazil's President toured <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and several countries in Central America and the Caribbean to promote Brazilian ethanol technology.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) that the American and Brazilian presidents signed in March 2007 may bring Brazil and the United States closer on energy policy, but it is not clear whether there has been substantive progress implementing the three pillars found in that agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Embrapa_gana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Embrapa_gana.jpg/220px-Embrapa_gana.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Embrapa_gana.jpg/330px-Embrapa_gana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Embrapa_gana.jpg/440px-Embrapa_gana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="1763" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Embrapa_gana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/EMBRAPA" class="mw-redirect" title="EMBRAPA">EMBRAPA</a>'s African Regional Office in <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brazil has also extended its technical expertise to several African countries, including <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ElPais_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElPais-177">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> This effort is led by <a href="/wiki/Empresa_Brasileira_de_Pesquisa_Agropecu%C3%A1ria" class="mw-redirect" title="Empresa Brasileira de Pesquisa Agropecuária">EMBRAPA</a>, the state-owned company in charge for applied research on agriculture, and responsible for most of the achievements in increasing sugarcane productivity during the last thirty years. Another 15 African countries have shown interest in receiving Brazilian technical aid to improve sugarcane productivity and to produce ethanol efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-ElPais_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ElPais-177">[177]</a></sup> Brazil also has bilateral cooperation agreements with several other countries in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>As President <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Lula</a> wrote for <a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> regarding Brazil's global agenda:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Brazil’s ethanol and biodiesel programmes are a benchmark for alternative and renewable fuel sources. Partnerships are being established with developing countries seeking to follow Brazil’s achievements—a 675m-tonne reduction of greenhouse-gas emissions, a million new jobs and a drastic reduction in dependence on imported fossil fuels coming from a dangerously small number of producer countries. All of this has been accomplished without compromising food security, which, on the contrary, has benefited from rising agricultural output...We are setting up offices in developing countries interested in benefiting from Brazilian know-how in this field."</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_social_impacts">Environmental and social impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Environmental and social impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">Issues relating to biofuels</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits">Benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ethanol produced from sugarcane provides energy that is renewable and less carbon intensive than oil. Bioethanol reduces air pollution thanks to its cleaner emissions, and also contributes to mitigate <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> by reducing greenhouse gas emissions.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_balance">Energy balance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Energy balance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_energy_balance" title="Ethanol fuel energy balance">Ethanol fuel energy balance</a></div>
<p>One of the main concerns about bioethanol production is the <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">energy balance</a>, the total amount of energy input into the process compared to the energy released by burning the resulting ethanol fuel. This balance considers the full cycle of producing the fuel, as cultivation, transportation and production require energy, including the use of oil and fertilizers. A comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle assessment">life cycle assessment</a> commissioned by the State of São Paulo found that Brazilian sugarcane-based ethanol has a favorable energy balance, varying from 8.3 for average conditions to 10.2 for best practice production.<sup id="cite_ref-MLA_2004_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_2004-12">[12]</a></sup> This means that for average conditions one unit of fossil-fuel energy is required to create 8.3 energy units from the resulting ethanol. These findings have been confirmed by other studies.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ethanol_104-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethanol-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WBImpacts_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBImpacts-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/220px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/330px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg/440px-BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="949" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BioethanolsCountryOfOrigin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/UK" class="mw-redirect" title="UK">UK</a> estimates for the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_intensity" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon intensity">carbon intensity</a> of bioethanol and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UKRTFO_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKRTFO-184">[184]</a></sup> As shown, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> ethanol from sugarcane is the most efficient biofuel currently under commercial production in terms of GHG emission reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_emissions">Greenhouse gas emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_fuel_standard" title="Low-carbon fuel standard">Low-carbon fuel standard</a></div>
<p>Another benefit of bioethanol is the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions as compared to gasoline, because as much carbon dioxide is taken up by the growing plants as is produced when the bioethanol is burnt, with a zero theoretical net contribution.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Several studies have shown that sugarcane-based ethanol reduces greenhouse gases by 86 to 90% if there is no significant land use change,<sup id="cite_ref-MLA_2004_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_2004-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ethanol_104-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethanol-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UKRTFO_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKRTFO-184">[184]</a></sup> and ethanol from sugarcane is regarded the most efficient biofuel currently under commercial production in terms of GHG emission reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WBImpacts_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBImpacts-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, two studies published in 2008<sup id="cite_ref-Science08_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science08-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fargione08_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fargione08-162">[162]</a></sup> are critical of previous assessments of greenhouse gas emissions reduction, as the authors considered that previous studies did not take into account the effect of <a href="/wiki/Indirect_land_use_change_impacts_of_biofuels#Direct_and_indirect_land_use_change_effects" title="Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels">land use changes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Times08_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times08-186">[186]</a></sup> Recent assessments carried out in 2009 by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA)<sup id="cite_ref-GCC0509_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0509-187">[187]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> (CARB)<sup id="cite_ref-SFG042409_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SFG042409-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT042409_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT042409-189">[189]</a></sup> included the impact of indirect land use changes (ILUC) as part of the lifecycle analysis of crop-based biofuels. Brazilian sugarcane ethanol meets both the ruled <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_fuel_standard#California_Low-Carbon_Fuel_Standard" title="Low-carbon fuel standard">California Low-Carbon Fuel Standard</a> (LCFS) and the proposed federal <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Fuel_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable Fuel Standard">Renewable Fuel Standard</a> (RFS2), despite the additional carbon emissions associated with ILUC.<sup id="cite_ref-GCC0509_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0509-187">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GreenM_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenM-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SACBee_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SACBee-191">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNICA01_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICA01-192">[192]</a></sup> On February 3, 2010, EPA issued its final ruling regarding the RFS2 for 2010 and beyond,<sup id="cite_ref-EPArule2010_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPArule2010-135">[135]</a></sup> and determined that Brazilian ethanol produced from sugarcane complies with the applicable 50% GHG reduction threshold for the advanced fuel category.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA2010_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2010-13">[13]</a></sup> EPA’s modelling shows that sugarcane ethanol from Brazil reduces greenhouse gas emissions as compared to gasoline by 61%, using a 30-year payback for <a href="/wiki/ILUC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">indirect land use change (ILUC) emissions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMom_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMom-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> By September 2010 five Brazilian sugarcane ethanol mills have been approved by the EPA to export their ethanol in the U.S. under the advanced biofuel category.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>A report commissioned by the United Nations, based on a detailed review of published research up to mid-2009 as well as the input of independent experts worldwide, found that ethanol from sugar cane as produced in Brazil "<i>in some circumstances does better than just "zero emission." If grown and processed correctly, it has negative emission, pulling CO2 out of the atmosphere, rather than adding it.</i>" In contrast, the report found that U.S. use of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> for biofuel is less efficient, as sugarcane can lead to emissions reductions of between 70% and well over 100% when substituted for gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-UN1009_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN1009-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNEP2009_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP2009-197">[197]</a></sup> A 2010 study commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> found that emission reduction effects of first-generation biofuels are positive, even after discounting <a href="/wiki/ILUC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">indirect land use change effects</a>, particularly the "more emission-efficient" sugarcane ethanol from Brazil, which would have to be imported to assure the environmental viability of the EU's biofuels mandate.<sup id="cite_ref-EUiluc_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUiluc-198">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EUilucPDF_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUilucPDF-199">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCCEU_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCCEU-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another 2010 study published by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> found that "<i>Brazil's transport sector has a lower carbon intensity compared to that of most other countries because of its widespread use of ethanol as a fuel for vehicles</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank2010_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank2010-201">[201]</a></sup> The study also concluded that despite the already low <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a>, urban transportation is responsible for 51% of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions within the Brazilian transport sector in 2008, and mainly originate in the growing use of private cars, <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">traffic congestion</a> and inefficient <a href="/wiki/Public_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a> systems. Nevertheless, the study concluded that the increased use of <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a> and the switch from gasoline to sugarcane ethanol are expected to stabilize <a href="/wiki/GHG_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="GHG emissions">GHG emissions</a> from the light vehicle fleet over the next 25 years despite an expected increase in the number of kilometers traveled. Furthermore, the study found that if bioethanol's market share of the gasoline-powered vehicle market reaches 80% in 2030, this switch from gasoline "<i>could deliver more than one-third of total emissions reduction targeted for the transport sector over the period</i>" (2008–2030). The study also concluded that by increasing Brazilian ethanol exports to attend the increasing international demand for <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_fuel_standard" title="Low-carbon fuel standard">low-carbon fuels</a>, its trade partners will benefit from reduced CHG emissions. However, for this opportunity to be realized, <a href="/wiki/Trade_barrier" title="Trade barrier">trade barriers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subsidies" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsidies">subsidies</a> in many countries will have to be reduced or eliminated.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank2010_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank2010-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2009 study published in Energy Policy found that the use of ethanol fuel in Brazil has allowed to avoid over 600 million tons of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions since 1975, when the <a href="/wiki/Pr%C3%B3-%C3%81lcool" class="mw-redirect" title="Pró-Álcool">Pró-Álcool Program</a> began. The study also concluded that the neutralization of the carbon released due to <a href="/wiki/ILUC#Direct_and_indirect_land_use_change_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">land-use change</a> was achieved in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-GreenMom_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMom-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergyPolicy_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergyPolicy-202">[202]</a></sup> In another estimate, <a href="/wiki/UNICA,_Brazil" title="UNICA, Brazil">UNICA</a>, the main Brazilian ethanol industry organization, estimated that just the use of ethanol fuel in <a href="/wiki/Flex-fuel_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Flex-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel vehicles</a> in Brazil has avoided 83.5 million tons of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions between March 2003 and January 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Air pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The widespread use of ethanol brought several environmental benefits to urban centers regarding <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>. <a href="/wiki/Leaded_gasoline" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaded gasoline">Lead additives to gasoline</a> were reduced through the 1980s as the amount of ethanol blended in the fuel was increased, and these additives were completely eliminated by 1991. The addition of ethanol blends instead of lead to gasoline lowered the total <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO), <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> emissions, and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate matter</a> significantly.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH3_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH3-204">[204]</a></sup> The use of ethanol-only vehicles has also reduced CO emissions drastically. Before the <i>Pró-Álcool</i> Program started, when gasoline was the only fuel in use, CO emissions were higher than 50 g/km driven; they had been reduced to less than 5.8 g/km in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> Several studies have also shown that <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="São Paulo (city)">São Paulo</a> has benefit with significantly less air pollution thanks to ethanol's cleaner emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH3_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH3-204">[204]</a></sup> Furthermore, Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flex-fuel</a> engines are being designed with higher <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratios</a>, taking advantage of the higher ethanol blends and maximizing the benefits of the higher oxygen content of ethanol, resulting in lower emissions and improving fuel efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-TRS08_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRS08-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even though all automotive <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> emit aldehydes, one of the drawbacks of the use of <a href="/wiki/Hydrated" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrated">hydrated</a> ethanol in ethanol-only engines is the increase in <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehyde</a> emissions as compared with gasoline or <a href="/wiki/Gasohol" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasohol">gasohol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH3_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH3-204">[204]</a></sup> However, the present ambient concentrations of aldehyde, in São Paulo city are below the reference levels recommended as adequate to human health found in the literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> Other concern is that because <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a> emissions are significantly higher, and although both aldehydes occur naturally and are frequently found in the open environment, additional emissions may be important because of their role in smog formation. However, more research is required to establish the extent and direct consequences, if any, on health.<sup id="cite_ref-TRS08_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRS08-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG/220px-Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG/330px-Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG/440px-Sugarcane_harvesting_equipment_Piracicaba_05_2009_5845.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
