</div>
</div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_use_and_fertilizers">Water use and fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Water use and fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Ethanol production has also raised concerns regarding water overuse and pollution, soil erosion and possible contamination by excessive use of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WBImpacts_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBImpacts-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nature06_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature06-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EcoSocial_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EcoSocial-207">[207]</a></sup> A study commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> government in 2006 to evaluate the sustainability of Brazilian bioethanol concluded that there is sufficient water to supply all foreseeable long-term water requirements for sugarcane and ethanol production. Also, and as a result of legislation and technological progress, the amount of water collected for ethanol production has decreased considerably during the previous years. The overuse of water resources seems a limited problem in general in São Paulo, particularly because of the relatively high rainfall, yet, some local problems may occur. Regarding water pollution due to sugarcane production, <a href="/wiki/Embrapa" class="mw-redirect" title="Embrapa">Embrapa</a> classifies the industry as level 1, which means "no impact" on water quality.<sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>This evaluation also found that consumption of agrochemicals for sugar cane production is lower than in citric, corn, coffee and soybean cropping. Disease and pest control, including the use of agrochemicals, is a crucial element in all cane production. The study found that development of resistant sugar cane varieties is a crucial aspect of disease and pest control and is one of the primary objectives of Brazil’s cane genetic improvement programs. Disease control is one of the main reasons for the replacement of a commercial variety of sugar cane.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Field_burning">Field burning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Field burning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Advancements in fertilizers and natural pesticides have all but eliminated the need to burn fields.<sup id="cite_ref-Nature06_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature06-206">[206]</a></sup> Sugarcane fields are traditionally burned just before harvest to avoid harm to the workers, by removing the sharp leaves and killing snakes and other harmful animals, and also to fertilize the fields with ash.<sup id="cite_ref-YahooUK_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YahooUK-208">[208]</a></sup> There has been less burning due to pressure from the public and health authorities, and as a result of the recent development of effective harvesting machines. In the mid 90s, it was very common to experience quite dense ash rains in cities within the sugarcane's fields during harvest seasons. A 2001 state law banned burning in sugarcane fields in São Paulo state by 2021,<sup id="cite_ref-UNICA2014_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICA2014-209">[209]</a></sup> and machines will gradually replace human labor as the means of harvesting cane, except where the abrupt terrain does not allow for mechanical harvesting. However, 150 out of 170 of São Paulo's sugar cane processing plants signed in 2007 a voluntary agreement with the state government to comply by 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-UNICA2014_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICA2014-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GovSAO_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GovSAO-210">[210]</a></sup> Independent growers signed in 2008 the voluntary agreement to comply, and the deadline was extended to 2017 for sugar cane fields located in more abrupt terrain.<sup id="cite_ref-YahooUK_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YahooUK-208">[208]</a></sup> By the 2009/10 harvest season more than 50% of the cane was collected in São Paulo with harvesting machines.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters0810_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters0810-211">[211]</a></sup> Mechanization will reduce pollution from burning fields and has higher productivity than people, but also will create unemployment for these seasonal workers, many of them coming from the poorest regions of Brazil. Due to mechanization the number of temporary workers in the sugarcane plantations has already declined as each harvester machine replaces about 100 cane cutters a day and creates 30 jobs including operators and maintenance teams.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters0810_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters0810-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_land_use_change">Effects of land use change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Effects of land use change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indirect_land_use_change_impacts_of_biofuels" title="Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels">Indirect land use change impacts of biofuels</a></div>
<p>Two studies published in 2008 questioned the benefits estimated in previous assessments regarding the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions from sugarcane-based ethanol, as the authors consider that previous studies did not take into account the <a href="/wiki/ILUC#Direct_and_indirect_land_use_change_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">direct and indirect effect of land use changes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Science08_157-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science08-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fargione08_162-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fargione08-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Times08_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times08-186">[186]</a></sup> The authors found a "biofuel carbon debt" is created when Brazil and other developing countries convert land in undisturbed ecosystems, such as <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannas</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> production, and to crop production when agricultural land is diverted to biofuel production. This land use change releases more <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> than the annual greenhouse gas (GHG) reductions that these biofuels would provide by displacing fossil fuels. Among others, the study analyzed the case of Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a> being converted for sugarcane ethanol production. The biofuel carbon debt on converted Cerrado is estimated to be repaid in 17 years, the least amount of time of the scenarios that were analyzed, as for example, ethanol from US <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> was estimated to have a 93-year payback time. The study conclusion is that the net effect of biofuel production via clearing of carbon-rich habitats is to increase CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for decades or centuries relative to fossil fuel use.<sup id="cite_ref-Fargione08_162-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fargione08-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding this concern, previous studies conducted in Brazil have shown there are 355 million ha of arable land in Brazil, of which only 72 million ha are in use.<sup id="cite_ref-Veja_30_04_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_30_04-154">[154]</a></sup> Sugarcane is only taking 2% of arable land available,<sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup> of which ethanol production represented 55% in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Folha_04_08_88-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Folha_04_08-88">[88]</a></sup> Embrapa estimates that there is enough agricultural land available to increase at least 30 times the existing sugarcane plantation without endangering sensitive ecosystems or taking land destined for food crops.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup> Most future growth is expected to take place on abandoned <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a> lands, as it has been the historical trend in São Paulo state.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup> Also, productivity is expected to improve even further based on current biotechnology research, genetic improvement, and better agronomic practices, thus contributing to reduce land demand for future sugarcane cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup> This trend is demonstrated by the increases in agricultural production that took place in São Paulo state between 1990 and 2004, where <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a>, sugarcane and other food crops were grown in an almost constant area.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also regarding the potential negative impacts of land use changes on carbon emissions, a study commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> government concluded that "<i>it is very difficult to determine the indirect effects of further land use for sugar cane production (i.e. sugar cane replacing another crop like soy or citrus crops, which in turn causes additional soy plantations replacing pastures, which in turn may cause deforestation), and also not logical to attribute all these soil carbon losses to sugar cane.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> Other authors have also questioned these indirect effects, as cattle pastures are displaced to the cheaper land near the Amazon.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Times08_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times08-186">[186]</a></sup> Studies rebutting this concern claim that land devoted to free grazing cattle is shrinking, as density of cattle on pasture land increased from 1.28 heads of cattle/ha to 1.41 from 2001 to 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup> and further improvements are expected in cattle feeding practices.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>A paper published in February 2010 by a team led by Lapola from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kassel" title="University of Kassel">University of Kassel</a><sup id="cite_ref-Lapola2010_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapola2010-214">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mongabay_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongabay-215">[215]</a></sup> found that the planned expansion of biofuel plantations (sugarcane and soybean) in Brazil up to 2020 will have a small direct land-use impact on carbon emissions, but <a href="/wiki/ILUC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILUC">indirect land-use changes</a> could offset the carbon savings from biofuels due to the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangeland</a> frontier into the Amazonian forests, particularly due to displacement of cattle ranching.<sup id="cite_ref-Mongabay_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongabay-215">[215]</a></sup> "<i>Sugarcane ethanol and soybean biodiesel each contribute to nearly half of the projected indirect deforestation of 121,970 km2 by 2020, creating a carbon debt that would take about 250 years to be repaid using these biofuels instead of fossil fuels</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-Lapola2010_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapola2010-214">[214]</a></sup> The analysis also showed that intensification of cattle ranching, combined with efforts to promote high-yielding oil crops are required to achieve effective carbon savings from biofuels in Brazil, "<i>while still fulfilling all food and bioenergy demands.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-Lapola2010_214-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapola2010-214">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mongabay_215-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongabay-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main <a href="/wiki/UNICA,_Brazil" title="UNICA, Brazil">Brazilian ethanol industry organization</a> (UNICA) commented that this study and other calculations of land-use impacts are missing a key factor, the fact that in Brazil "<i>cattle production and pasture has been intensifying already and is projected to do so in the future.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Deforestation">Deforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Deforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg/220px-Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg/330px-Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg/440px-Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="896" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goldemberg_2008_Brazil_sugarcane_regions_1754-6834-1-6-1_Fig_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Location of environmentally valuable areas with respect to sugarcane plantations. <a href="/wiki/State_of_S%C3%A3o_Paulo" class="mw-redirect" title="State of São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, located in the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Southeast Region, Brazil">Southeast Region of Brazil</a>, concentrates two-thirds of sugarcane cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldemberg_84-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldemberg-84">[84]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other criticism have focused on the potential for clearing rain forests and other environmentally valuable land for sugarcane production, such as the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_biome" title="Amazon biome">Amazon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pantanal" title="Pantanal">Pantanal</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Cerrado" title="Cerrado">Cerrado</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Science08_157-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science08-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oxfam_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfam-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fargione08_162-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fargione08-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Times08_186-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times08-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EcoSocial_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EcoSocial-207">[207]</a></sup> Embrapa and UNICA have rebutted this concern explaining that 99.7% of sugarcane plantations are located at least 2,000 kilometres (1,200&#160;mi) from the Amazonia, and expansion during the last 25 years took place in the Center-South region, also far away from the Amazonia, the Pantanal or the Atlantic forest. In São Paulo state growth took place in abandoned pasture lands.<sup id="cite_ref-ExpansaoCana_97-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ExpansaoCana-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergiaCana_98-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergiaCana-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Does_Sugarcane_Threaten_the_Amazon_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Does_Sugarcane_Threaten_the_Amazon-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>The impact assessment regarding future changes in land use, forest protection and risks on biodiversity conducted as part of the study commissioned by the Dutch government<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> concluded that "<i>the direct impact of cane production on biodiversity is limited, because cane production replaces mainly pastures and/or food crop and sugar cane production takes place far from the major biomes in Brazil (Amazon Rain Forest, Cerrado, Atlantic Forest, Caatinga, Campos Sulinos and Pantanal).</i>" However, "<i>...the indirect impacts from an increase of the area under sugar cane production are likely more severe. The most important indirect impact would be an expansion of the area agricultural land at the expense of cerrados. The cerrados are an important biodiversity reserve. These indirect impacts are difficult to quantify and there is a lack of practically applicable criteria and indicators.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to guarantee a sustainable development of ethanol production, in September 2009 the government issued by decree a countrywide agroecological land use zoning to restrict sugarcane growth in or near environmentally sensitive areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Pantanal" title="Pantanal">Pantanal</a> <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> and the Upper <a href="/wiki/Paraguay_River" title="Paraguay River">Paraguay River</a> Basin.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ZAECana_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZAECana-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VEJA0909_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VEJA0909-220">[220]</a></sup> The installation of new ethanol production plants will not be permitted on these locations, and only existing plants and new ones with environmental licensed already approved before September 17, 2009, will be allowed to remain operating in these sensitive areas. According to the new criteria, 92.5% of the Brazilian territory is not suitable for sugarcane plantation. The government considers that the suitable areas are more than enough to meet the future demand for ethanol and sugar in the domestic and international markets foreseen for the next decades.<sup id="cite_ref-ZAECana_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZAECana-219">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VEJA0909_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VEJA0909-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_implications">Social implications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Social implications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trabalho.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Trabalho.JPG/220px-Trabalho.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Trabalho.JPG/330px-Trabalho.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Trabalho.JPG/440px-Trabalho.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trabalho.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical sugarcane worker during the harvest season, São Paulo state.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sugarcane has had an important social contribution to some of the poorest people in Brazil by providing income usually above the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>, and a formal job with fringe benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH12_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH12-221">[221]</a></sup> Formal employment in Brazil accounts an average 45% across all sectors, while the sugarcane sector has a share of 72.9% formal jobs in 2007, up from 53.6% in 1992, and in the more developed sugarcane ethanol industry in São Paulo state formal employment reached 93.8% in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH12_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH12-221">[221]</a></sup> Average wages in sugar cane and ethanol production are above the official minimum wage, but minimum wages may be insufficient to avoid poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> The North-Northeast regions stands out for having much lower levels of education among workers and lower monthly income. The average number workers with 3 or less school years in Brazil is 58.8%, while in the Southeast this percentage is 46.2%, in the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">Northeast region</a> is 76,4%.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH12_221-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH12-221">[221]</a></sup> Therefore, earnings in the Center-South are not surprisingly higher than those in the North-Northeast for comparable levels of education.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> In 2005 sugarcane harvesting workers in the Center-South region received an average wage 58.7% higher than the average wage in the North-Northeast region.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH12_221-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH12-221">[221]</a></sup> The main social problems are related to cane cutters which do most of the low-paid work related to ethanol production.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The total number of permanent employees in the sector fell by one-third between 1992 and 2003, in part due to the increasing reliance on mechanical harvesting, especially in the richest and more mature sugarcane producers of São Paulo state. During the same period, the share of temporary or seasonal workers has fluctuated, first declining and then increasing in recent years to about one-half of the total jobs in the sector, but in absolute terms the number of temporary workers has declined also.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> The sugarcane sector in the poorer Northeast region is more labor-intensive as production in this region represents only 18.6% of the country's total production but employs 44.3% of worker force in the sugarcane sector.<sup id="cite_ref-MacedoCH12_221-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacedoCH12-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>The manual harvesting of sugarcane has been associated with hardship and poor working conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EcoSocial_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EcoSocial-207">[207]</a></sup> In this regard, the study commissioned by the Dutch government<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> confirmed that the main problem is indeed related to manual cane harvesting. A key problem in working conditions is the high work load. As a result of mechanization the workload per worker has increased from 4 to 6 ton per day in the eighties to 8 to 10 ton per day in the nineties, up to 12 to 15 ton per day in 2007. If the quota is not fulfilled, workers can be fired.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> Producers say this problem will disappear with greater mechanization in the next decade.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT100406_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT100406-5">[5]</a></sup> Also, as mechanization of the harvesting is increasing and only feasible in flat terrain, more workers are being used in areas where conditions are not suitable for mechanized harvesting equipment, such as rough areas where the crops are planted irregularly, making working conditions harder and more hazardous.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also unhealthy working conditions and even cases of slavery and deaths from overwork (cane cutting) have been reported,<sup id="cite_ref-EcoSocial_207-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EcoSocial-207">[207]</a></sup> but these are likely worst-case examples.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> Even though sufficiently strict labor laws are present in Brazil, enforcement is weak.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup> Displacement and seasonal labor also implies physical and cultural disruption of multifunctional family farms and traditional communities.<sup id="cite_ref-EcoSocial_207-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EcoSocial-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding <a href="/wiki/Social_responsibility" title="Social responsibility">social responsibility</a> the ethanol production sector maintains more than 600 schools, 200 nursery centers and 300 day care units, as legislation requires that 1% of the net sugar cane price and 2% of the net ethanol price must be devoted to medical, dental, pharmaceutical, sanitary, and educational services for sugar cane workers. In practice more than 90% of the mills provide health and dental care, transportation and collective life insurance, and over 80% provide meals and pharmaceutical care. However, for the temporary low wage workers in cane cutting these services may not be available.<sup id="cite_ref-SusDutch_102-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SusDutch-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_food_prices">Effect on food prices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Effect on food prices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food vs fuel">Food vs fuel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/220px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/330px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG/440px-Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2176" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Faz_S_Sofia_canavial_090607_REFON.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Santa Sofia sugarcane field for ethanol production in <a href="/wiki/Avar%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Avaré">Avaré</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Environmentalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmentalists">environmentalists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/George_Monbiot" title="George Monbiot">George Monbiot</a>, have expressed fears that the marketplace will convert crops to fuel for the rich, while the poor starve and biofuels cause environmental problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Monbiot_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monbiot-222">[222]</a></sup> Environmental groups have raised concerns about this trade-off for several years.<sup id="cite_ref-Times08_186-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times08-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food vs fuel">food vs fuel</a> debate reached a global scale in 2008 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a>'s concerns regarding the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">steep increase in food prices</a>. In April 2008, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ziegler" title="Jean Ziegler">Jean Ziegler</a>, back then <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Special_Rapporteur" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Special Rapporteur">United Nations Special Rapporteur</a> on the Right to Food, called biofuels a "<i>crime against humanity</i>",<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Brasilia_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Brasilia-227">[227]</a></sup> a claim he had previously made in October 2007, when he called for a 5-year ban for the conversion of land for the production of biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Also in April 2008, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s President, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zoellick" title="Robert Zoellick">Robert Zoellick</a>, stated that "<i>While many worry about filling their gas tanks, many others around the world are struggling to fill their stomachs. And it's getting more and more difficult every day.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a> gave a strong rebuttal, calling these claims "<i>fallacies resulting from commercial interests,</i>" and putting the blame instead on U.S. and European <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">agricultural subsidies</a>, and a problem restricted to U.S. ethanol produced from maize. The Brazilian President has also claimed on several occasions that his country's sugar cane–based ethanol industry has not contributed to the food price crises.<sup id="cite_ref-Econon06_08_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Econon06_08-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veja_30_04_154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_30_04-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>A report released by Oxfam in June 2008<sup id="cite_ref-Oxfampdf_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfampdf-234">[234]</a></sup> criticized biofuel policies of rich countries as neither a solution to the climate crisis nor the oil crisis, while contributing to the food price crisis. The report concluded that from all biofuels available in the market, Brazilian sugarcane ethanol is "far from perfect" but it is the most favorable biofuel in the world in term of cost and greenhouse gas balance. The report discusses some existing problems and potential risks, and asks the Brazilian government for caution to avoid jeopardizing its environmental and social sustainability. The report also says that: <i>"Rich countries spent up to $15 billion last year supporting biofuels while blocking cheaper Brazilian ethanol, which is far less damaging for global food security."</i><sup id="cite_ref-Oxfam08_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfam08-235">[235]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> research report published in July 2008<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank_07_08_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank_07_08-237">[237]</a></sup> found that from June 2002 to June 2008 <i>"biofuels and the related consequences of low grain stocks, large land use shifts, speculative activity and export bans"</i> accounted for 70-75% of total price rises. The study found that higher oil prices and a weak dollar explain 25-30% of total price rise. The study said that <i>"...large increases in biofuels production in the United States and Europe are the main reason behind the steep rise in global food prices"</i> and also stated that <i>"Brazil's sugar-based ethanol did not push food prices appreciably higher."</i><sup id="cite_ref-Veja_28_07_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veja_28_07-238">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-239">[239]</a></sup> The report argues that increased production of biofuels in these developed regions were supported by subsidies and tariffs on imports, and considers that without such policies, price increases worldwide would have been smaller. This research paper also concluded that Brazil's sugar cane–based ethanol has not raised sugar prices significantly, and recommends removing tariffs on ethanol imports by both the U.S. and EU, to allow more efficient producers such as Brazil and other developing countries, including many African countries, to produce ethanol profitably for export to meet the mandates in the EU and U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank_07_08_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank_07_08-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>An economic assessment report also published in July 2008 by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a><sup id="cite_ref-OECD_07_08_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_07_08-240">[240]</a></sup> agrees with the World Bank report regarding the negative effects of subsidies and trade restrictions, but found that the impact of biofuels on food prices are much smaller. The OECD study is also critical of the limited reduction of GHG emissions achieved from biofuels produced in Europe and North America, concluding that the current biofuel support policies would reduce greenhouse gas emissions from transport fuel by no more than 0.8% by 2015, while Brazilian ethanol from sugar cane reduces greenhouse gas emissions by at least 80% compared to fossil fuels. The assessment calls on governments for more open markets in biofuels and feedstocks in order to improve efficiency and lower costs.<sup id="cite_ref-OECDweb08_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECDweb08-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study by the Brazilian research unit of the <a href="/wiki/Funda%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Fundação Getúlio Vargas">Fundação Getúlio Vargas</a> regarding the effects of biofuels on grain prices.<sup id="cite_ref-FGV_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FGV-242">[242]</a></sup> concluded that the major driver behind the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 rise in food prices</a> was speculative activity on <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">futures markets</a> under conditions of increased demand in a market with low grain stocks. The study also concluded that expansion of biofuel production was not a relevant factor and also that there is no correlation between Brazilian sugarcane cultivated area and average grain prices, as on the contrary, the spread of sugarcane was accompanied by rapid growth of grain crops in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FGV_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FGV-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:758px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg" class="image" title="Panoramic view of the Costa Pinto Production Plant located in Piracicaba, São Paulo state. This industrial plant is set up to produce sugar, ethanol fuel (both anhydrous and hydrous), industrial grade ethanol, and alcohol for beverages. The foreground shows the receiving operation of the sugarcane harvest, immediately followed by the mill process, and in the right side of the background is located the distillation facility where ethanol is produced. This plant produces the electricity it needs from baggasse residuals from sugar cane left over by the milling process, and it sells the surplus electricity to public utilities."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg/750px-Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg" width="750" height="367" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg/1125px-Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="578" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panorama_Usina_Costa_Pinto_Piracicaba_SAO_10_2008.jpg" title="File:Panorama Usina Costa Pinto Piracicaba SAO 10 2008.jpg"></a></div>
Panoramic view of the Costa Pinto Production Plant located in <a href="/wiki/Piracicaba" title="Piracicaba">Piracicaba</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">São Paulo state</a>. This industrial plant is set up to produce sugar, ethanol fuel (both anhydrous and <a href="/wiki/Hydrous" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrous">hydrous</a>), industrial grade ethanol, and alcohol for beverages. The foreground shows the receiving operation of the sugarcane harvest, immediately followed by the mill process, and in the right side of the background is located the distillation facility where ethanol is produced. This plant produces the electricity it needs from <a href="/wiki/Baggasse" class="mw-redirect" title="Baggasse">baggasse</a> residuals from sugar cane left over by the milling process, and it sells the surplus electricity to public utilities.</div>
</div>
</div>

<li><cite class="citation book">Macedo, Isaias de Carvalho, editor (2007). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.unica.com.br/multimedia/publicacao/"><i>A Energia da Cana-de-Açúcar – Doze estudos sobre a agroindústria da cana-de-açúcar no Brasil e a sua sustentabilidade</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(Available in PDF)</span> (in Portuguese) (Second ed.).
